GAME: Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
[First impressions]
Many, many aspects are crammed around a simple (at its core) system, giving it much detail and an ample variety of options during each turn. However, I must also say that my first experience with this game has been far from great. 
Hannibal seems to be simply too long for the amount of luck it brings into play. Having the luck of the draw to influence an hour long game is one thing, but when we shift gear to a 4 hour title it's a completely different matter. I'm sure that playing time can be reduced with repeated play, but even getting to the advertised 150 minutes can't change the nature of the problem. The huge swings of luck can put you back on game after a false start, but they also ruin your plans in a way that doesn't really fit with our idea of "inevitable unknown factors that can influence a war".
The battle system is just a match of risk taking while you try to exploit the limits of your rival's hand of cards. The problem is that it hardly feels like a battle: only a couple of cards have some kind of effect on the battle itself and its consequences, the other cards could easily show a number instead of a battle manoeuvre. Just a row of text would have sufficed: some kind of modifier on the following rounds or other ways to react to the cards of your rival, instead of ending up losing a battle with a huge advantage of cards because you didn't have *that* card. Is this supposed to simulate those desperate battles with unpredictable outcomes? Ok, but I'd really like to have a part in that, instead of thanking (or blaming) my luck.
After all we're talking about a game that has two tables for casualties calculation, and at least a couple of exceptions on almost every rule. I can understand that many of those exceptions and design choices have their justification, along with the occasional historical flavour, but the gap between that and the combat system is considerable. 
Going back to the rulebook after the game made me notice how much space is taken by rules that come into play just once in the game, at most. On the other hand, you have three different retreat rules concerning cities, that hardly compare with the couple of simple paragraphs about political influence, one of the main paths to victory.
I also find difficult to define this game as being "easy to learn". I'm perfectly aware that there are lots of wargames out there that would make this rulebook look like a leaflet, but there are still lots of rules in there, without an index and not always well organized. Add the time needed to learn how to play this game properly to the already long playing time and you end up with an experience that, I'm afraid, is not my cup of tea.
It's a game that mixes brilliant design choices to a few annoying shortcomings. I can see how it can occasionally really shine, but I'm afraid it's not worth the time investment on the way to that "big, perfect play". Of course, I'll give it another try, but so far I'm more impressed by the design than by the game itself.

RATING: 10
This is one of those games whose legend precedes it.  It's postion as a wargaming super-star is almost akin to that of Hannibal himself in generalship.  What I've discovered over the dozen plays I've had so far, is that this reputation is justified, for this is a game worthy of endless replaying and which is full of huge subtleties and depths.  And yet somehow the rules, though long at the first read, click together pretty nicely and the whole thing seems constantly very rational.  Of course, the War covered is one of the most famous in history and I must say that the designers got it about right in their interpretation.  

I particularly like the way the card system can be used to do so many things and that it is much harder to move as well as fight with certain generals.  The naval rules are simple but elegant and the battle cards keep you on the edge of your seat. So far, I have detected a slight advantage to the Carthiginian player, but there are those who say the opposite...so what do I, a mere beginner know?  I look forward to playing this game scores of times! My fourth play moved this up from a 9.5 to a 10 and I haven't looked back on that since. A great game, but I know it's costly to obtain these days.  The forthcoming reprint will bring a whole new army of fans to this gem of a game.  If you have any interest in wargaming, if I were you, I'd grab it.

In most of the games I have played, we ended it at turn 6, when Scipio Africanus entered play, as it at that point was very clear which side was winning.

RATING: 10
A timeless Classic and outstanding GREAT GAME!

Has all that is needed to simulate this epic period: Elephants, Syracuse, Politics, Consular elections, Diplomacy, Legions, Treachery, Navy, Campaigns to cut the breath, cunning battle tactics, Plagues, Alps, Gauls and ... much, much more.
As the second CDG (after We the People), Hannibal RvC has far exceeded its predecessor.
For me, is the best CDG game that combines the wonderful historical flavour with replaybility in a very clean and simple way.
A constant presence in our gaming tables; we never get tired of play it. 

Playing time: Between 2 or 4 hours

RATING: 5
Hannibal has its good points: while the rules are at my limit in terms of length, they are easily assimilated.  I found the interaction of war and politics quite cleverly done, particularly the draining away of political support after a military defeat. The asymmetry of the two sides is interesting; the Carthaginians with a great leader and lots of good political cards, but short of men and handicapped by Roman control of the seas; the Romans with a really mixed bunch of leaders, but endless reserves of men.  I even like the tension that the maligned battle cards bring.

The Valley Games edition is handsome, though - and here the criticisms start - I find the board artwork rather cluttered and lacking in functionality.  It took a long, hard look to spot the dotted straits of Messana. The borders of Italian provinces could also have been clearer.  

However, my main difficulty lies with the limited strategic options open to Carthage.  A Fortress Spain and North Africa strategy might work, but would be very boring.  Sending Hannibal over the Alps is much more fun but very risky if the Roman has the sense to throw everything at him. In addition, Hannibal's implementation of area control feels crude and dull compared to what euros have made of the genre.

RATING: 7

It is a very subtle difference, and i think very much down to personal taste. This is a good game but not for everyone
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For the second Card-Driven Wargame - and regarded as a classic - this must be a great game, but now I am acquainted with many other CDWs I have mixed feelings.

It is still a very decent game from many respects, but there was room for improvement. I realize this isn't easy to achieve for Valley Games, since you'll have a different game then. Reissuing is the easier way and I am grateful for that.
 
The battle system is a nice attempt but doesn't give you a battle feeling. Dice rolling would have saved time. Furthermore the game swings back and forth too much. Both the Romans and Carthaginians - with the right cards - can land almost anywhere.

The game doesn't force or encourage Hannibal to cross the Alps and bring destruction to Italy. You can use him to defend Spain and wait for opportunities to come in the end game. More scripting was desired by me.

At last this game doesn't reward courage. You can win by going the safe way - one province more than your opponent - instead of the total victory as it historically was.

RATING: 8
An excellent, and relatively simple, introduction to card-driven wargaming.  What sets it apart from other games of its type (other than We the People) is the way combat is resolved.  The card-based combat mechanic works in this game but can be problematic.  Shuffling and drawing cards for a blowout (Hannibal attacks small roman garrison) is a waste of time.  But the combat system does allow for some interesting situations and outcomes that can't be found using a standard dice-based CRT.

But it is undoubtedly with the result that this play is appreciated as much by all the public ones!

RATING: 8
A great two player wargame.  The two sides are asymmetric and play differently.  The event cards make each game fresh and challenge you to spot opportunities in each hand.  And the game does a great job of building a unique narrative.

The drawbacks are that the rules are poorly designed, and that while most cases are covered, occasionally you can run into a unique problem that the rules are confusing or just ambiguous about.  And while it's far from a luck driven game, there can be wild swings of luck in card draws or critical rolls.

For both of those reasons, I try to play the game a little less competitively.  And that works, because the narrative nature of this game and the opportunities presented in each new hand of cards keeps things enjoyable even when you are losing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The good: very interesting theme, very good "combat" mechanics, a lot of small decisions that pile up to be a strategy.
The bad: very long, many parts, pretty difficult to "save status" (although I did it once and it did work)

Bottom line: highly recommended for people who are into long war games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Hrm, down goes another sacred cow for me.  I've only played the VG production, which I found excellent despite my common pre-order issues.  Very clear and legible rulebook, nice art, etc.  I heard that the areas used to be denoted on the cards in the old version, which I'd prefer just to not have to ask where ZZZ is or stare in its general direction and blow my hand in the process due to inexperience.  The rules are fine and not too heavy, just a lot of modifiers.  The consul/proconsul stuff took away more than it added IMO.  I didn't mind and possibly even liked the battle card system, to modest surprise.  My biggest issue is that I felt it was an overwrought game of reversi.  Any game can be distilled and belittled like that, but in this case I don't think the abstractions on top of it did anything to further the case of the game.  It just felt like the game got in the way of our flipping counters from one side to the other.  Just to give an example, I'm not the biggest fan of Twilight Struggle but I don't feel that way about its control changes which are remarkably similar - call that stronger success of its theme and implementation, at least for my tastes.

RATING: 9
Excellent game, great atmosphere, great graphics. The card based battles are a fine break from odds tables and give one the feeling of having some control over one's armies and having decisions to make beyond rolling dice. Rome has a slight advantage in the game, but both sides are a blast to play with different strategies for each. A classic.

Occasionally excessive card luck prevents it from being a 10.

The Romans, in particular, can sometimes get hosed by bad luck. For instance, it is possible to be dealt a devastatingly bad hand of stratagy cards that make it virtually impossible to do anything for a turn (while your opponent can operate with virtual impunity). Perhaps a greater abundance of 2 or 3 value cards, or greater flexibility in use was in order. Or home cards such as in the GMT multiplayer CDG's.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Végre kipróbálhattam a kártyavezérelt műfaj egyik úttörőjét! HATALMAS ÉLMÉNY VOLT! (Megjegyzés: ez volt a 400-dik játék, amit próbáltam, jó kis jubileum volt:)) Bár nem teljesen ugyanaz az alapmechanizmus, mint a TS-ben és az 1960-ban, vannak elemek, amik ott is visszaköszönnek. Röviden összehasonlítva: a három közül ez a legfajsúlyosabb darab szerintem, és talán a legjobb is?!

Kezdjük a legegyszerűbb és legvitathatatlanabb szemponttal: a játék gyönyörű! A puzzle-szerűen összerakott tábla, a vezetők, a jelölők... a szebb fajta wargame-ek közé sorolják a játékot. (Wargame-ek körében  elég nagy a szórás ezen a téren...)
A szabály bonyolultsága elviselhető, viszonylag gyorsan elmondható, főleg ha valaki játszott már más kártyavezérelttel. A kártyák nyelvfüggőek, kb. annyira, mint az összes többi kártyavezéreltben. Ami hátrány: 6 óra tisztán:)

De elszalad a 6 óra, mert a játék nagy élmény tényleg. Forulatos, teljesen átélhető. Az első 2-3 körben még nem láttam át teljesen, és ekkor úgy is éreztem, hogy tuti kikapok a punokkal. Szegény Hannibál Kr. e. 216 körül meghalt. Teljesen vesztésre álltam. Utána a Hasdrubal nevű vezetőm megtáltosodott, és kb. 15 ellenséges egységet likvidált, megölte Scipio Africanust is. A két legfontosabb személy amint játékba került, meghalt. Az 5-6. körtől kezdtem úgy érezni, hogy van esélyem (a döntetlen már karthagói győzelmet jelent). Sokáig 10-8 volt a területek aránya a javamra, majd 9-9-on stabilizálódott az arány. De a 8. menetben váratlanul partra szálltak a rómaiak Afrikában, és két hazai tartományomat befoglaltak. Vége szakadt a nyerő szériának, és pont akkor kerültem hátrányba, amikor már csak 1 év volt hátra. Ekkor mindent egy lapra tettem fel, és nekimentem Rómának (ha elfoglalom a várost, azonnal nyerek), de nem jött be, és végül nyert Róma.
Nem szoktam ilyen részletesen leírni egy konkrét partit (önálló blogban szokás ilyesmit), de szerintem jól érzékelteti, hogy mennyire életszerű, mennyire megfordítható a veszettnek hitt parti, és ami így leírva nehezen adható vissza: végig van egy hangulata, és ami fontos, egy lassan változó íve. Majdhogynem egy utólag elmesélhető sztori az egész. Ez a TS-ben pl. nincs meg. Ott ezernyi felé dobáljuk fel a lapkákat, puccsolgatunk jobbra-balra, túlságosan a kártyák alakítják a játék menetét. Itt nem érzem azt, hogy annyira rátelepszenek a kártyák a játékra: alakítják a taktikánkat, de mégis kialakulhat egy vezérfonal. Egyébként nem érzem hogy durván erős kártyák lennének, és egy kártya átlagban 2-3-szor játékba kerül, tehát elég jól kiegyenlítődnek az esélyek. 
A kocka helyett külön kártyapaklival vívjuk meg a csatát, nekem ez nagyon tetszik, mint megtudtam, a lassúsága miatt hagyták ki a későbbi hasonló játékokból ezt a mechanizmust. A valósághűség kedvéért játékba kerülő proconsulok is ötletesek.

10 oldalt is tudnék írni, de kár tovább ragozni: az egyik legjobb játék, amit eddig próbáltam. Nem az a fajta, ami után felugrik az ember, hogy "na de király volt, jöhet egy visszavágó?", hanem olyan, aminek hatása alatt vagyok, de órák óta nem tudom összeszedni rendesen, hogy miért:)  
És bár kicsit kapkodóan játszottam, de ezzel együtt azt érzem, hogy akár sakk mélységű pozíciós harc is kialakulhat két tapasztalt játékos között. Nyerő stratégia? Ugyan már:)
---
Módosítom a véleményemet annyiban, hogy vannak durván erős kártyák a játékban, és ebből fakadóan elég fontos a kártyák ismerete. Új játékossal javasolt végigmenni a 64 stratégiai kártyán játék előtt, mert néhány területet ezek ismeretében fokozottan érdemes védeni. Emellett fontos a hadvezérek képességeivel nagyon tisztán lenni, nagyon nem mindegy, hogy melyik a jó hajós, a jó védekező, jó támadó, jó ostromló, stb... Ez a rész nagyon nagy változatosságot visz a játékba.
A második partim vége nagyon fordulatos volt. Alapesetben összesen 144 lap kerül kijátszásra egy játék során, a mi játékunkban a 142. lap után Karthagó állt nyerésre 9-9-el, a 143. lap után Róma javára módosult az állás 8-9-re, a 144. lap után pedig újra Karthagó volt a nyerő 9-8-ra, ezzel meg is nyerte a játékot...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Of all the leader based card driven wargames, Hannibal without a doubt works the best. The political aspect is well integrated with the military aspect, and it means you've always got stuff to do with your ops. Everything about Hannibal is pretty much spot on, it doesn't have too much or too little. The playing time is right, the complexity is reasonable, the unit density isn't overwhelming, the thematic integration is excellent, there's a good sense of tension and decision making, both players get to play some offense and defense, there are multiple ways to win, the balance is pretty good and there isn't too much scripting. I do have a few concerns. One, Carthage is very fragile. You have to scavenge with them and really play the system. That leads to some learning curve due to some counter-intuitiveness and also some gamey tactics. Two, the luck of the draw really is a big deal here. The game is all about delaying and using your last plays to steal territories, but he who draws Messenger Intercepted, the Revolts, the Diplomacy cards, etc is at a big advantage. In fact, I'd go so far to say that Carthage's entire game is based around this sort of thing since they can't win a head on fight in the long run. Third, the game really can stall out in unsatisfying ways with the roadblock strategy. I think I may ultimately prefer We the People to Hannibal due to the mentioned roadblock issue and Hannibal's longer playing time (3 hours), but I can't argue that the systems in Hannibal are much more in sync with each other. And it's definitely got an epic feel for a card driven game, with good emphasis on both unit maneuvering and hand management.

RATING: 8.5
Comments after intial play.

Well, all the hype that I have been subjected to did not disappoint. I really quite liked the experience. There is a simplicity to the rules that I like. Furthermore, I found that the relatively broad expanse of the map allowed for a number of thoughtful decisions; yet essentially being able to narrow those plans down to three theatres with satellite areas. That helps the newbie a wee bit. The play was thought provoking without being a strain over the 5+ hours of playtime. All in all, I am anxious for more plays.

And the battle cards! Well, they were fun. A bit of a game within a game. 

PROS: Challenging situation with what appears to be some replayability. Fun decisions without being weighty or onerous. The card events are interesting and enhance the feel of the subject. Battle cards provide a game within a game.

CONS: The battle cards can slow things down a bit. Initial session ran to 5.5 hours which I think can be reduced to about 4...maybe. Too long for a weekday evening?

RATING: 10
Now that I have finally had a chance to play it, I can say that the game is fantastic.  I own the Valley Games edition, and it is a top notch production using any standard (I can't compare it to AH as I've never seen it).  The game is a lot of fun and I really enjoy the use of cards for the battle.  It does drag some battles out, which can make it inordinately long for PBEM, but it is actually way more fun than the "bucket of dice" alternative.  The single roll variants may speed up play - especially useful if playing solitaire - but removes all of the drama in the close fights.  The strategy here is clever and close games can go down to the wire.  It is hardly a free for all in combat as Carthage's replenishments are just not sufficient to outlast a determined Rome.  Carthage must play the game of controlling spaces, while Rome needs to exact pain at every opportunity right up to the end.

Playing it online via ACTS (good card management, terrible map management), wargameroom (very well done, live play only) as well as VASSAL and CB.  Valley Games has breathed life back into this game and kudos to them plus the old guard for such an entertaining game.

RATING: 9.5
De los mejores CDGs y parcticamente el mejor para iniciarte.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
UPDATE: Quick Take on the new edition: In general, I like the new edition. It's not perfect, and ultimately isn't overall as nice as the old AH edition, but it's very solid and excellent in some areas. What I like about the new edition: great box, good and attractive rules, the puzzle-board is great and gets you a hgh-quality board without the usual hazards of puzzle-boards (it's a vastly better solution than the "deluxe" GMT boards), and I love the Roman and Carthaginian dice. And assuming I get them at some point, I'll like the plastic generals. What's not so hot: CG graphics are over-used, there are a number of unattractive cards that belong in a computer game, not a board game; they might have been better sticking with the old line drawings. The battle cards aren't great; they're noisy and static, and the disruptive purple band breaks the color scheme of the game in my opinion (plus, as purple is a color associated with Rome, it seems to give the battle cards a bias). The important info on the strategy cards (the number) is too small. What I think was positively bone-headed: the numbers on the CUs are *way* too small. Come on, this is not rocket science. Even GMT got this right. And switching to hexagonal PCs (instead of round ones) boggles the mind; the Cartheginian PCs and tribe markers are graphically much too similar - I have absolutely no idea why this switch was made, the only rational answer I can come up with is that for some reason an appropriate circular die was unavailable or too expensive.

Overall the production is aggravating because where it's good, it's really good, but they dropped the ball in just enough ways to make the new edition not as good as the old one. The font problem with the CUs is the most glaring, but the overuse of CG and the hexagonal PCs are problematic as well. The old edition had its shortcomings, but it was more cohesive and much more usable. I vent about this becuase its always frustrating when a reprint, in which much was re-done and much was done extraordinarily well, is still actually overall inferior to the original because of a few obvious shortcomings. I am reminded of the Brazillian Modern Art, which was so nice in so many ways, but then for some bizarre reason dropped the wonderful, descriptive, internationalized auction symbols that every previous edition had used. 
---
Although only the second game in the genre of We The People-inspired games (as of 7/02, this numbers ~8 games!), Hannibal is really the system at its peak - most of the other games try to shoehorn the system into a game that doesn't quite fit it, to varying degrees of success (PoG is probably the best of the rest, althouh it is also the most significant overhaul of the basic game). Hannibal also represents, for me, a near-perfect design as a game; everything that is included has a significant purpose, the design is clean and coherent, the game has an ease of learning that belies the depth of the game, and the historical flavor is tremendous without dominating the game a la Richard Berg or acting as a straightjacket. This is a fairly complex game when compared to the modern, lighter multi-player fare, but anyone who is not too complexity-averse should try this game even if not generally into historical or simulation gaming.

RATING: 9.5
This is my favorite wargame. Simple. Balanced. Lots of excitement and dramatic turn of events. Wonderful portrayal of the 2nd Punic War. An absolute classic.

Some people object to the battle cards. I think they're great! I wish that some day some newer CDG titles will also employ this mechanism.

Further, it is nice to have a wargame actually having a mounted map. The map look and the cards cannot compare to present-day GMT or Phalanx offerings, but I guess this is state-of-the-art design for an Avalon Hill game (and looks reasonably good). I wish though that Descartes had done more with the German edition I own.

In any case, this game really NEEDS to be reprinted (preferably with better production values). I hear that at least English language versions now cost a fortune at Ebay. Phalanx Games should really get in touch with HASBRO (if they're the ones with the rights) and release a new edition. 

For me, the King of the Card-Driven Wargames (and I'm a big fan of those). And a perfect introduction to the wargame genre as a whole. 

-----
Minor criticisms (and the reason why I'm not giving Hannibal a 10): I believe the Romans are overly encouraged to turn Italy into an iron fortress in the early turns and stacking lots of units there rather than undertaking risky overseas expeditions. I've posted a variant of mine which adresses this concern.

Another point which somewhat bothers me is the relatively low importance of the cities. Considering  the effort involved in besieging the non-capital cities, the payoff is IMO too slim. I've equally posted a variant adressing this - though it may be a tad too unforgiving.

RATING: 9
Lots of difficult decisions every turn. Needs to be played several times to appreciate the depth and to fully grasp the rules. It's quite long (about 4+ hours assuming that neither Rome nor Carthage fall) but the assymetrical nature of this game is fascinating and I'm always surprised how quickly the time goes. About 30 plays to date.

Avalon Hill vs Valley Games versions: The Valley Games reprint is very nice. The jigsaw board is excellent (mine sits flat anyway) as are the counters. The Roman and Carthaginian dice are a nice novelty but I'm not sure that they are totally fair and prefer to use something a bit heavier. The plastic insert holds the components pretty well. The card backs of the strategy and battle cards are confusingly similar and the art on the strategy cards was obviously computer generated and isn't as pleasing as the original Avalon Hill line drawings. Unfortunately the rules aren't totally comprehensive so it is necessary to check the forums for many minor rules questions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
An excellent game, my respect for it grows with each session.  While it lacks the elegance of its father, "We the People", it has greater depth and is more robust.  Hannibal's rules have more chrome, but the additional time spent learning them pays tremendous dividends.

For example, armies that loose battles can retreat over their opponent's PC markers (unlike WtP), but they loose a unit of strength per PC marker traversed, and must end their retreat in a friendly controlled space.  So there are more rules, but not too excessively so, and the result is one of the best gaming experiences to be had. 

At its core, the game is all about Political Control.  But the rulebook is organized in turn order, so the Political Control rules are relegated to section 19, and the game's most central aspect is not stressed as well as one might hope.  Newbies should read section 19 before diving into the rulebook - as an understanding of PC and the way it pervades all other aspect of the game, combat, movement, victory .. is essential for success and enjoyment.

The Valley Games reprint is much appreciated and reasonably well produced but far from flawless.  The board is graphically *way* too busy - to the point of obscuring important game information.  The colors used in Italy are too dark, making it difficult to see regional boundaries and transit routes.  The strength value on the combat units and the Ops value on strategy cards two of the game's most critical pieces of information, are too small.  The linen texture of the counters and board is very slippery, leaving counters sliding about the board during play.  And as a sort of coup de grace, the Carthaginian six sided die is a waste of perfectly good wood.  Valley Games seems to be cutting out a niche based on gorgeous games with graphic choices that obscure or negatively impact important game information.

Given this game's reputation and the reprint's affordability, it is definitely worth trying.

RATING: 7
Initial rating after 3 plays. I own the Valley Games edition.

This a good game. It is thematic (rating +1), the sides are asymmetric (rating +1) the gameplay is tense with many interesting choices (rating +2).  Unfortunately it is too long: 5-7 hours, especially considering the amount of luck involved. There is a big element of luck in battle: what cards are dealt, who gets the initiative, the retreat losses roll etc. You can lose even if odds seem to be strongly in your favour. I would be OK with that – the battle system gives the game a distinctive feel – if the battle luck was balanced at the strategic level. Unfortunately there is nothing at the strategic level that mitigates a loss of full 10 unit stack due to bad luck in a single battle: an event that can cost you the game, especially if you are playing Carthage. Moreover, the game is also random at the strategic level, e.g. some cards are always useful but some are rather situational. (overall rating -2 for the excessive luck). Finally, reshuffling the battle deck is a chore.

The board and pieces look nice (rating +1) but there are issues of functionality. For instance, borders of provinces and connections between spaces do not stand sufficiently out in Italy after the placement of political control markers. Or consider the unit tokens: the number on the unit tokens is too small and different on the sides leading to mistakes, the tokens also do not stack well: overall the use of locking plastic chips of different colours (e.g. Modern Art chips) would have worked much better. (rating -1 for lack of functionality). The rules (including card instructions) are reasonably well written but could clearer, especially considering that this a reprint of a classic game. Some cards do not mention all the effects, even though there is room on the card for extra text. Very regrattably there are two errors in the information printed on the board (modifier for crossing non-alps pass, the special ability of Fabius)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Wowza. The rules to this game (VG edition, arrived at long last) are [i]tough[/i]. Not as in difficult, but as in 'copious'. And wargamers think that these are average in terms of complexity...? Closest masochists, the lot of them.

My initial play against my significant other of just 3 rounds was a sobering experience with many mistakes made and learned from. Like: 'Don't have Hannibal walk into Italy without a hidey-hole to retreat to'. Hannibal is a powerful general, true, but he [i]can[/i] be beaten and Carthage should only risk the odds if the odds are [i]heavily[/i] in his favour. Another don't is 'Land a Roman army squat in the middle of Hispania when that area is blue as far as the eye can see'. A powerful do is 'Major Campaign cards are very useful in saving multiple strategy cards for it allows you to move multiple generals with just one card'. All in all, it's a lot more ad hoc and uncertain than I imagined from reading the rules: this is a Good Thing. I disliked Revolution intensely because it [i]didn't[/i] have a lot of uncertainty to work with—unfortunately, I cannot at this moment say with any certainty whether the randomness of the cards is 'too much' or 'just right' or simply 'okay'.

There has been considerably discussion about the battle cards: despite me being an absolute rookie, I'm not sure I like them a great deal. The game is the strategy cards; playing a dozen different cards in an 'I think that you think that I think ...'-subgame feels a bit off for some reason. I could live very easily with a simpler system or just plain dice like, for example, found in Mare Nostrum. Yes, I know, Hannibal should have not [i]actually[/i] have something like in MN: I'm merely using it to illustrate that simple systems are not always a Bad Thing™. The reason for me preferring dice is their speed: a couple of throws, and you're done. Next strategy card, please! Of course, you'd lose out on an important tactic: withdrawal.

But this is all coming from an absolute rookie of course, so don't pay him any attention :-). Nevertheless, this game is serious business, and should be treated as such. I think the diversity with the cards will pull it through in the end; now it's just table time which it desperately requires so you're not buried in a rule book all the time.

[b]Update[/b] Hannibal turned out to be... well, a bridge too far, I guess the most fitting expression is. It's not really an issue of play time, as a game lasting as long will find a way to my table if it is fun enough. No, the problem was the voluminous rules with (paraphrasing here) exceptions to subsubsubclauses. Games lasting this long are only feasible at my table if people 'get' the rules easily and quickly; and Hannibal fails on this account. I wish it were different, but I guess I'm simply not sufficiently hardcore to cope. Hannibal was, quite simply, not a game for us. I let it go, a little wiser about where our boundaries are.

And the rate for this game? To be perfectly honest, in hindsight I doubt it would rise above a 5.

RATING: 9
I like the asymmetry here. I like how every card has an event, rather than some just being usable for OPs, as in Washington's War. The game is historically accurate without too much rules overhead. Combat is cumbersome at times, and I have yet to play a game without referencing the rules. Still, it is a wonderful game with many difficult decisions for each side. 

Although the card-based combat system lengthens the game significantly, I'm now okay with it, even though I still think a nice CRT would be great as an alternative. There's incredible tension from the playing of battle cards, and you can't really mimic that with a die roll.

Naval combat has been abstracted away with simple negative modifiers to a die roll for success/failure of transporting armies via ports. For Carthage, that is. Rome had naval superiority during that time and can sail at will (well, still needs a 3 Ops card to do so).

After 16 plays, I'm finally comfortable with the rules (both sides) and can knock out a game with another experienced player in about 3.5 hours with minimal rules referencing. The more I play it, the better it gets. It's just an amazing design, both from an historical and gameplay standpoint. Hannibal is such a badass--but you can't be too bold with him. I have yet to risk sailing with him, and even when he marches into Italy, you have to make sure to keep him near Carthaginian control markers so he has a place to retreat to, in the unlikely event he loses a battle or (more likely) withdraws. Getting Hannibal killed will make things much more difficult for the Carthaginian player, and should be the Roman player's goal.  

P.S. I really don't care that I don't own the generals minis. The cardboard tokens work just fine, and are just as immersive. 

Playing Carthage is difficult for a newbie, I find. Rome is easier to play well as a new player. No need to worry about naval travel failure. With Carthage, I rarely risk sailing until I have a -4 or -5 modifier to the die roll. 

We play with the variant rule regarding the Messenger Intercepted event--the player playing the event must immediately take another action. 

Holy crap are there some glaring errors in both the rulebook (VG) and the mapboard. Crossing a non-Alps pass s/b -2 modifier, Fabius' ability should read simply "avoid battle on a 1-5", and the regions of Italy are difficult to distinguish. Several spelling mistakes on the board and/or general tokens (i.e. Flaminus s/b Flaminius), Macedonia misspelled on the Naval Chart, and "general" misspelled on the "Rome Dismisses Proconsul" strategy card. I would love to see GMT publish this.

RATING: 10
Great game, great theme. Simple mechanics, yet lots of tough decisions to make with the dual op/event cards. The battle card & initiative system and the tie-in with leadership is brilliant. Minimal chrome. A relatively small deck size means every card will be seen at some point during the game, even if it is by your opponent, so you typically generate some sort of coherent narrative.

The complaints about the battle card system should be ignored, especially from the whiny "takes too long" euro crowd. Ancient warfare was usually won by the better general, even at 1:2 odds. The cards, leaders, and initiative system tie that all together in an easy, natural way. Combat systems using a single die roll cannot reasonably simulate (or even approximate) ancient battles, and are completely unsatisfactory.

I can't say I'm a fan of the new puzzle board from Valley Games but that's a minor complaint.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[More than 10 plays on-line]

Awesome, truly fantastic game! There are few games out there that really deserve their classic status but I feel Hannibal is one of them. It's easy to teach and play without becoming too simplified and silly.

The game focus on the struggle between Carthage and Rome in the 2nd Punic war with all what that entails. Hannibal of course, elephants, marches across the Alps, tense battles, attrition, political control of provinces and more. The most amazing thing is that everything works; nothing in the game feels forced or out of place. Despite the length of the rulebook it's a very clear system that allow very few gamey moves if any.

Hannibal being a card driven game means you'll have several tough decisions to make with every card play; activate a general, build up some political support, build troops or play the card for its event. The cards also makes certain that no two games will be alike.

My only minor gripe with it is that the card draws can ruin the game on some occasions. There are cards that give one player several activations in a row and that can be a game winner at the right moment. It usual evens out in the end but having several of your messengers intercepted in the same game certainly makes you gnash your teeth. We usually play with the optional rule for "Messenger Intercepted" found in the VG edition rules.

Update: Sold my old German version and bought the new VG one. Excellent stuff! Of course preordered the new Phalanx edition as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage was a bit of a revelation for me, tying together multiple wargames threads that I enjoy into a complete, satisfying package. It is one of the first games in a long, long time that I have instantly rated a 9 and have found a place in my Top 10. It is a simply a great design. What makes it so great? Well, I will tell you. 

1) Differentiated Leaders. Maybe it is my role-playing game background talking, or my desire to simulate the fact that some commanders were better than others, but I appreciate the fact that different leaders have different statistics and abilities. While it might have been slightly better to separate a leader’s stats into three areas (battle rating, agility, and strategy rating), how they do it is good enough, particularly when you add in the special abilities of each leader.

2) Events and Operations on one card. My introduction to card-driven games came from 1960, Here I Stand, and Twilight Struggle so I am used to having my events and ops combined into one card package. The fact that Hannibal uses this, rather than the inferior events and operations are separate card types of We the People/Washington’s War, is greatly appreciated. I wonder why they didn’t do some work and incorporating this superior development when they republished We the People as Washington’s War?

3) A neat mini-game. Hannibal uses a battle card system to resolve combat, where each player is given a hand of cards, the size of which is based on a number of factors, and where each player plays a card to attack, which the defending player must then match. Then the defending player has an opportunity to seize the offensive. If the player does, then they are able to play the leading card, if they fail, then they must continue matching the attacker. The first person to be unable to match the played card loses the battle. While I appreciate the speed of dice-based resolution mechanics, the battle system of Hannibal is pretty fun. The combination of bluffing and hand management produces building tension and fun in a way that a simple die roll can not.

4) A game of politics, where battles are an extension of this political battle. I am fascinated by both history and politics so the fact that the majority of the card driven games I have played so far (Hannibal, Here I Stand, Washington’s War, and Twilight Struggle) are at their heart political games is something that I greatly enjoy. Games that are purely focused on wars are generally less interesting to me unless they are games focused on individual battles. What I particularly like about Hannibal’s implementation of this idea is the fact that winning and losing battles matter. Every time you lose a battle you have to remove influence markers based on the amount of men you lose. This is something that was absent from the other CDG games that I have played, and makes total sense. Battles can have significant repercussions in the support of local leaders, and the fact that you can lose that support, and thus have to spend influence to regain it is an excellent design decision

5) Antiquity. In euros and economic games I tend to have only a minor interest in theme. As long as the game is good, I am willing to get past ludicrous or pasted on themes. War games are different, and it takes a very well regarded design for me to get past thematic disinterest enough to play it. So my favorite war game theme is ancients, and Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage is clearly ancients themed. Win. 

There is more to it than that, but if go into any greater detail, I would have to turn this into a review. As it stands, I am tempted to write one anyway, but it will have to wait until I have played it more than twice. Still, it’s a great game and if you like Washington’s War and are okay with a slight increase in complexity and play time, then Hannibal is a great step up. 

Edit: On further play, I have started to find the game to feel a bit repetitive, I am not sure though if that is due to my frequent opponents or simply due to the game. As it stands, I am lowering the game's rating to an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Players play either Rome or Carthage, and battle out the 2nd Punic War.  Players play cards to move troops around the point to point mapboard, or to trigger events.  The ultimate objective is to establish political control in the various provinces, and the use of force is simply a tool to achieve that end.  We The People started it, Hannibal took it to the next step.  In some ways this is my favorite of the "series" with the perfect mix of complexity and playability.  The two sides play pleasantly differently, as the Carthage player frantically pulls every trick in the book to try and hold back the crushing power of Rome.

RATING: N/A
Played for the first time.

This is one amazing simulation game I'd say. Of how it resembles and managed to capture the history and put it into play is simply fascinating. I was intimidated at first (I'm sure my opponent as well; we were taught by someone else) with all the rules and what can you do in your turn, thus missing some opportunities to place the Political Counters (PCs) on the open spot on certain provinces.

After only two to three rounds (a round takes quite a while, probably 30-40 minutes) I've managed to stream down of all the options available, but I realized it was too late. Carthage army already breached into Roman (now Italy) wall with his ground units (foot and elephant units) coming all the way from Spain.

The battle is pretty interesting as well. Depending on certain factors, both sides will be given battle cards that is taken from the same deck. The attacker will play a card (usually showing a battle/attack formation) and the defender needs to play the same exact card that matches the attacker card. If you can't, you simply lost. If you do, you have the chance to counter attack by rolling a die.

This game uses interesting mixtures of card driven mechanics and some dice rolling to check for attrition, retreat, sea exploration and such (so there are few tables you need to refer to, which are all displayed on the board).

Another thing to appreciate is how gorgeous the map is. It was an amazing experience, one that I will never forget. Although 4 hours of gaming and knowing that I've lost at the end, was a little disappointed. But it's okay, I always take my first game to be a learning game.

Long Live Roman Empire!

RATING: 4
[2 plays] Unfortunately, this is too much of a "grind" game for me.  The battle card system and combination OPs/Event cards should really appeal over a Herman-style CDG, but the game is ultimately too tedious.  There's no tension between the ops/event choices.  

All you can do is but butt heads and gain that extra inch through brute force.  Clever moves (if there is such a thing in Hannibal) are inherently risky and the game obviously favors one side or the other depending whether Africanus is out, so there's little need to be clever in the first place.  There's so little contested political space, entire hands are quickly spent flipping a couple PC markers or nothing at all if you're really unlucky as the Romans.  

I find nearly every other CDG I've played to be so much more dynamic than Hannibal.  I'm surprised it's still so highly regarded considering how one dimensional it feels.  Most other CDGs have other layers of conflict on top of the board and many future considerations - deck management, multiple decks, seeded events, event tracks, etc.  It's all on the table from the beginning in Hannibal, but worse, I feel no tension.  Nothing convincingly pulls me in multiple directions.  I don't feel like I have any flexibility to sacrifice now for the greater good later.  It's just grind, grind, grind.

Update: The new edition incorporates the errata and is printed on a large jig-saw puzzle type board. The game is still a blast. It's simply the best two-player CDG I've ever played. No 2 games are the same after the first turn and it's always nail-biting time. Just consider it Twilight Struggle on steroids. If your game collection has more than 3 games, this has to be one of them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The beauty of Hannibal is that it is wide-open: there is a lot of room for strategic maneuver (about 100 map locations) and only a few units (typically each player will have no more than 3 or 4 locations that actually contain units at any given time).  So you have a few powerful stacks of units combined with a wide-open movement system (due to the use of sea movement, which always happens in every game).  It's so big and so sweeping - with grand-strategic events made possible through the creative play of card combinations that you receive 7, 8 and 9 at a time (depending on the game turn) that it can be really exciting and variable even within a game from turn to turn.

However, the flip-side of Hannibal's greatest strength is its weakness.  The possibility of wide-open grand-strategic change with just a few units in play makes it somewhat brittle - a characteristic that I have realized is true of nearly all of the card-driven war games. As a result games frequently do not go the distance: my experience is that only about every 3rd session of this type of game is actually fun.

When the cards come out in a fairly balanced way the game is easily a "10".  But most of the time: it's not nearly that good.

RATING: 8
[b][i]Hannibal[/i][/b] is a great war game. The card driven action system is simultaneously fun and includes many events from history (so be careful, you'll probably learn something). The asymmetric play is tense and rewarding; both sides have tremendous strengths and the winner will be the player who better utilizes their side's abilities. With multiple (at least 2 but often 3) uses for each card, every card is worthwhile.

My biggest complaint is the card driven combat system. It's fun, especially the first few times, but optimal plays are usually easy to determine for an experienced player. Still though, combat is quick and has the right amount of luck.

RATING: 8
[b]Hannibal: Rome vs Carthage[/b] [i]Mark Simmonovith 1996[/i]
Appart from Twilight Struggle, Hannibal is the only two players game in my collection. Reason is simple, with these two outstanding games I wont need any more in a while (given that many eurogames like Caylus or RftG work perfectly with two persons). Hannibal is a classic card driven game and probably it is the peak of the system. Battles dont use dice but a deck of combat cards, and movement and political control rules are easy to grasp, intelligent and fit perfectly with the theme. The new Valley Games mounted puzzle map is absolutely brilliant and the carthaginian dice add a very nice detail in such a neat edition. All in all, if you like two players complex games and the card driven mechanics this game will be a hit for you.

RATING: 8
Newbie to the game, bought the Valley Games reprint.  

This is a great game - lots of difficult choices to make.  The learning curve for the rules isn't too bad if you're used to wargame-type rules layouts, and I suspect it's shorter than the learning curve for strategy.Although it's styled as being a wargame, at its heart, this is really an area-control game, pre-empting by some years the popularity of that game mechanic in eurogames.  Yes, there are wargame elements that you don't typically see in a euro, but these are simplified relative to the norm for wargames.  The dropping of a few elements here and there, and this wouldn't look too different from some of the more advanced euro-fare out there.The renewed availability of this game, and the wider forum offered by BGG compared to what was available when the game was first published, should produce some interesting cross-polination with other euro mechanics and I look forward to seeing HRVC feeding the development of some more sophisticated and engaging euros in years to come.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I think it's been ten years since I last played this game, and while I was glad to get it back to the table, we were both a little disappointed. Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage was the first card-driven wargame that I played, and back in 1997 it blew me away. I loved facing difficult decisions with each play, I loved putting together a plan of action with each new hand, I loved how events were integrated into the game, I loved that a general could have the road wide open in front of him and yet still refuse to march. 

Now, years later, those virtues can be found in dozens of games, and so Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage is left to be judged on it's few remaining unique points. There's the subject, which is marginally interesting to me, and there's a chassis that is for the most part clean and fast-moving. But for all that, the game feels a little too long for what it offers, and for a comparable investment in time, I think I'd rather play Twilight Struggle. I am also not fond of a situation that generally rewards nibbling at the edges over taking direct action against your enemy.

Not a bad game by any stretch -- but no longer the titan it seemed when card-driven wargames were the bold new thing in wargaming. I do regret that almost every game that followed Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage found it necessary to be longer, heavier, and more baroque than the original designs.

The Valley Games reprint is very handsome, but it goes a theme too far with that ridiculous Carthaginian die. I kept rolling "P."

RATING: 9.99999
This game is fantastic!

With that out of the way I'll list my perceived problems and suggested fixes.

1) Problem: Multiple  occurrences of "Messenger Intercepted" by the same player can be a game ender.

Fix: Make it an alternating Event, once played by either player it is not playable as the Event by that player unless and until the other has played it as the Event.

Rationale: Mostly pure game balance but how realistic is it for one side to repeatedly lose its messengers who always spill the beans?


2) Problem: The Double Envelopment Battle Card favours low ranked generals. It is much more advantageous for Varro to play DE against Hannibal than the other way around since Varro can expect to lose initiative anyway.

Fix: Auto loss of initiative due to play of "Double Envelopment" occurs against an equal or higher rated general; if DE is played against a lower rated general who matches the DE card the lower rated general must then roll to gain the initiative as for any other matched Battle Card. Consider "Double Envelopment" to represent any Surprise or Unorthodox tactic that would require an equally agile opposing general to qualify for an automatic counter.

Rationale: Hannibal's signature tactic (at Cannae against Varro) is a difficult and dangerous maneuver best left to the experts who also possess superior cavalry.


3) Problem: The Roman long term game strategy frequently hinges on holding out for Scipio Africanus to appear, often creating a "Fortress Italy" mindset.

Fix: Roll 1d6 each turn starting Turn 2 at which time a d6 of 1 brings on Africanus with 1CU; on turn 3 he shows up on a d6 of 1-2 with 2CU; turn 4 on 1-3 with 3 CU; turn 5 on 1-4 with 4CU; turn 6 and later on 1-5 with 5CUs. The catch? Africanus with attendant bonus CU/s must appear with any existing army or CU/s _outside_ Italy. The roll is only made if there is at least one army/CU eligible to receive him and if so the attempt is mandatory. Roman choice of placement if two or more eligible spaces exist. Think of "Africanus" as a leader who managed to rise to prominence through accomplishments and not necessarily the historical Africanus, the way a specific Roman leader is replaced with the same counter if he is killed in combat or by storms at sea.

Rationale: Roman predilection for augury notwithstanding, the Romans at the time did not know they had a guaranteed "1-4" Leader scheduled to appear on "Turn 6" of what they did not expect to be an agonisingly protracted war and could thus play for time by stalling with an oversize stack of CUs at Mutina.  Conversely, Carthage has the knowledge that Hannibal will not face his equal until late in the game allowing mid-game strategies rather than believing, as Hannibal did, that the path to victory lay in the quick kill which Hannibal had expected after the massacre at Cannae. The historical Hannibal couldn't count on the Roman Senate's dogged determination to run through all available dolts before being forced to trot out a Rockstar like Africanus. Like the Arnold Traitor card in We the People the current rule supplies fixed information about an unknown subject, the effects here being even more perniciously influential than in WtP.


4) Problem: Have the perfect card . . . but it's not effective in the current turn.

Fix: Allow players to withhold one card at the end of a turn, the following turn the hand is filled to normal capacity and ALL cards must be played. 

Rationale: Can be a help mostly for Carthage in the first couple of turns with cards that need to be used when Hannibal is in Italy. Establishing a foothold in Italy is crucial for Carthage unless opting for the Sicilian Offense.

YMMV

RATING: 10
Deep, demanding, offering many possibilites.

I own the VG editions which - except for the illegible board (provinces, where are the provinces?) - is stunningly beautiful and of top-notch quality. The gameplay itself runs smoothly and rules are coherent and far from being convoluted. Rulebook is clearly written with plenty of examples.

Update 17th February 2010: the rate goes up from 8 to 9. Basically speaking, I really hate how random this game is, but I will be willing to play it any time. There are so many other aspects that I love, including maneuvering and interception, which enables player to create defensive strongholds over certain provinces, that my deep aversion for luck dependence, which is the drawback of all CDGs, is strongly surpassed.

I love this game, I repeat. If it wasn't to frustration two or three unlucky hands my cause, this would be a perfect 10. In the form as it is, it remains at 9. I would love to see this game refurbished, with improved mechanics.

6th April 2010: it is random, indeed, but careful hand management is also crucial. Additionally, few games can convey so much tension and replayability. Today I played my 9th game and found it to be my best game so far. Who knows how wonderful games await me in the future? Rating goes up to 10.

RATING: 3
Wow, this was a surprise.  I was really expecting to like this game, but it more than fell flat.  The strategy seems scripted, with Carthage crossing the Alps on the attack and Rome turtling up waiting for Scipio Africanus.

The battle cards are silly (but no sillier than a CRT, really), and significantly increase the game's length.  The strategy cards are generic and gamey.  The political-influence subgame is clumsy and thematically unconvincing.  Each of these subgames feels almost but not entirely disconnected from the others.  Taken as a whole, the game gives one the distinctly unpleasant experience of playing three rather uninspired games at once.

The Valley Games production is beautiful, but has a few serious graphic-design flaws.

Onto the trade pile it goes...

RATING: 9
I like card driven wargames. I understand that this is one of the early card driven classics. 

Each side plays very differently but the game is still finely balanced. It's fast moving. It's quite a brain burner - a well timed event card can be brutal. RATING: 9
A great game, deserving of the praise it gets.  The below comments were written at several different sittings.

This rating is (WAS) partially on potential, since the first time I played this, both of us were a little clueless on the strategy for the first turn or three, and it really effected the way the game went.  Rome was on the defensive for most of our game, and the Carthaginian player seemed to have luck on his side and things stayed that way through most of the game.  However, I can really see the potential as a sort of PoG light version.  This game packs a lot of the same punch that games like paths of glory and twilight struggle do, but can be played in less time and with a lot less complexity.  You get some of the same type of gripping decisions that PoG presents you with - fight hard on one front, or try to put the pressure on your opponent by fighting on multiple fronts, etc.  Quite a few times in our game well layed plans got screwed over by one general or another hopping on a boat and heading for an unguarded area of the board, prepared to lay political waste to the countryside or defeat smaller forces if left unchecked.

Strategy card draw effected the game, but luck there didn't seem too important.  The battle cards are a cute little mechanic, and add a real feeling of importance to every battle.  The allies rules seem to tilt things toward carthage a lot, but I think if the romans had been able to take a province in spain things would have been a little different.  

The production values are top notch, with my only complaint being that the dark colors of italy make the lines a little tough to see.  The dice and the chits are all great, and the board is beautiful.  I like the puzzle piece design on the board.  Mine was not warped at all.

Overall, I'm not sure where the rating on this one is going to settle.  I need to play it again to see if some of the negative things in the game were from us HRVC virgins making bonehead plays or if the game really has some little issues.  I think its probably more of the former.  


UPDATE:  I'm raising this up to a 9 from an 8.  The game seems to do a really good job of corralling non-historical decisions.  The rules seem to be geared toward this, but are not too blunt about it.  Its pretty damn clever and gives me new respect for the game.

2ND PLAY UPDATE:  Just played my second game of Hannibal.  This is a really great game.  This game was very close, until the last two turns when some bonehead plays by carthage (me) got some important armies SUNK, including Hannibal's.  You really need to develop a little lead on the Romans before Scipio Africanus shows up with his high ratings.  My first game, I took Hannibal with about 12 CUs into Italy and did very well.  This second game, I wrapped up northern spain faster so I took Hannibal over the alps with a only about 8 CUs instead of more.  I think in the long run this was a big mistake.  If Hannibal loses even one or two battles, he can really get screwed because of the reinforcement rates.  You need to wait and take Hannibal with a full complement into northern italy.  Hannibal with a full army will beat up on any of the non-africanus roman generals pretty consistently.  He can also do it fast enough to not take much attrition.  However, if you go in with less than a full force, Hannibal will be losing a man here and a man there and before too long you will take a big loss...  Not good!   

FINAL UPDATE: This is a fantastic masterpiece of asymetrical balance.

RATING: 9
Beautiful game.
The cardmechanic shares many similiarities with other CDGs like We the people/Twilight Struggle/Paths of Glory etc. But where Twilight Struggle is only about area control via clever card play Hannibal incorporates armies, battles & sieges.
The two sides are quite asymetrical, Carthage having the advantage early on mainly because Hannibal can be activated by 1 OP cards & then Rome catching up with much more reinforcements & Scipio Africanus entering on turn 6.
One of the best ways to resolve battles i must say is this card-combat system, because it gives the player control.. something that dice do not, while keeping the tention very high!

The map & artwork is fantastic (except for the italian provinces, they're very hard to distinguish)

The game is a very long one.. we're talking 5-6 hours. But those were the days.. ;)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Hannibal is the direct offspring of the wonderful We the People, set in the 2nd Punic Wars between Rome and Carthage.

Both sides have some very definite advantages, which play well off each other: Carthage starts with all generals on the board, including the eponymous Hannibal, who does to Rome what Washington does to the British.

However, while both Rome and Carthage have access to Naval Movement, for Carthage it is often a real risk to attempt it, because of Roman Naval superiority.

Rome has a random draw of politically appointed Consuls plus one Proconsul appointment, so they are limited to three generals for much of the game, until Scipio Africanus enters to equalize the military leader balance.

The political control system is even better then in We the People, requiring force to help turn the PC tide once the base has been set, unless a helpful event comes along.

On the whole, events seem the favor Carthage, but many are not usable unless they are in a position to hold sway in Italy.

This all makes for a tight, well-balanced game.

I had an incredible ending to my first game, with both Carthage and Rome besieged simultaneously, Carthage ahead on Political points, and only Scipio's facility in siegecraft making the difference as Carthage fell in the final round.

I loved the game- I had suspected that I would, but it surpasses the earlier success of We the People with something even better.

I am starting this at an 8, and could easily see it rise from there. This is truly a classic game; if you love Twilight Sentinel, you owe it to yourself to look at its direct precursor.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is comfortably the best of the early CDGs, but even this early in the system's development the number of rules and playing time were already being stretched in comparison to We the People. This is an object lesson in offering a card deck that truly drives the game, it's the flow of events that really determines how the war plays out. Note that few cards are events in the historical sense, many are generic with titles like 'Diplomacy' or 'Storms at Sea', but they remain important in the context of the game. Carthage can be rather fragile, it's only real asset is Hannibal himself, but with only one route into Northern Italy and troublesome Naval Movement rules, Italy can be off-limits to Carthaginian armies leading to the game degenerating into the notorious 'sitzkrieg'. The events can help here, but Carthage in particular must play the hand it's dealt. The Carthaginian die is cool but not really practical (it's also incorrect as opposite faces don't add up to 7). This is one of the few single deck games that truly gives opportunities to both sides without luck overwhelming good play, but this remains a design that I respect rather than enjoy. A variant map is my preferred way to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Lives up to the hype.

Very smart mechanics that do a terrific job of mimicking the dynamics of the 2nd Punic War.

It may be an odd analogy, but the conflict felt a bit like the American Civil War - Carthage (like the Confederacy) features superior military leadership, while Rome possesses better resources (manpower) and logistics via sea movement (like the Union).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I never imagined I would be really interested in this game. It's a war game and a long game (not by war game standards). The games I really like are 1 hour long or less, any other game I own takes at most 1.5 hours. This one, I believe, takes 3+ hours for a full game when both players know the rules rather well (longer if they don't) and has many more rules than the typical games I like, but, the game is really really good.

At its essence it's a game of area control. Luckily for me, instead of moving 50 units of various types and upgrades, the movement is done only with generals of which each side has 4. The events and dual use of the cards make for fascinating decisions. The battles themselves are intense - much better than throwing dice. Still, the game is not about battles and typically is not full of fighting. It's about controlling areas, positioning to keep the enemy away, seizing opportunities and using events wisely. I am amazed at how well it captures the story and theme of the second Punic war. It is not historically accurate, but that's a good thing as that would make the game more tedious, in my opinion. Instead, it is inspired by a true story captured here in so many intricate aspects of the gameplay mechanics and design. Examples include the use of elephants in battle, double envelopment, unique strengths of the various generals, many of the special events, attrition, battle and political casualties, Rome building slowly with more reinforcements, while Carthage has the initiative and is on the offense from the start but lacks long term reinforcement capabilities.

All of this makes for an extraordinary game.

I am surprised to have found a game so worthy without really looking for one. Potentially a personal 10, but I need to play this more before I find out. I am eager to do so, but the length and complexity make it a difficult task.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It's elegant, it offers lots of decision making and the card-play for tactical battles is brillant, so much more fun than just rolling dice on a CRT. Takes severals plays to fully grasp the potential of cards and strategies, after that it is a very balanced game. As a simulation, it has its limits, as a game this one is superb.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I love the way Hannibal RvsC's theme combines military with political aspects and the way the mechanics mix on-map counter movement with card play. The constant need to decide whether to use the strategy cards as events, to place PC markers or to move generals produces a tense game. Even the use of battle cards, while still an abstraction and not entirely satisfactory, is novel and gives a feeling of tactical control that is entirely absent in the more usual resolution of battles using dice rolls. Furthermore, Hannibal always plays out over the course of the game in such a way that both players are fully involved. If one player gains a major advantage then the game will probably end quickly. But if the advantage is minor then a win can never be guaranteed until the last moment.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.25
Looking VERY forward to this one...

Well an update at last. Finally got to play after 7 years of waiting and you know what, it lived up to its reputation!  TG

Needless to say, this is the shit. The highest I think I have ever rated a game after one play, usually 8.5 is my limit but I got a really good feel for this after just one play and it presses all the right buttons for me including long term planning amongst many other cool things. Not trying to be a wargame like many AC games do with a war theme, this is a wargame (somewhat of a sim too) where units and leaders are moving into other territories (can use the sea too) and causing havoc and having battles where damage is dealt out and retreats happen too, there is an AC aspect here also though. TS borrowed heavily here using the very same card system but left out some of the true war aspects which I really missed. 

The battle card system, well, lots has been said already. For me, better than dice (and a little like Taj Mahal actually) but still random mostly no doubt and you do roll for initiative which is quite important. You do get more cards for having more units though so this helps to give a stronger force a better chance of winning and often damage isn't too bad for a losing force but tables can turn either way for sure. It IS fun however and I like the way initiative changes hands and rolling under your leader determines this, very cool and makes the better leaders very important. Also the double surround card gives a bonus to damage but is risky to play because you can lose the initiative, liked that too. Rolling for the damage though with the +2 extra damage added, yikes, that can really break you since its possible to lose ALL your forces, a little harsh but then, so was war I suppose, and this wont happen too often (at least I think and hope!). Overall I think I like the card system mostly and also the combat system as a whole and MUCH better than just dice rolls for me. I also like the options you have too, got crappy cards, maybe you try to withdrawl from the combat and try your luck another day; don't want to fight at all, try and avoid the combat altogether, options are always good.  

Only played once but it seems like the overall luck in the game is in check here (unlike many wargames) and equally important is that you have many options on how to do things. There aren't any really big blows that will ruin you (except maybe losing all in a battle which will definitely sting and the sea can be harsh too but you know this and can avoid it when its too risky until later events make it more favourable) and mostly it seemed pretty balanced too. For sure the luck bothered me a little more in TS as a comparison (coup and realign rolls) but I have played that 6 times now and this once so we'll just have to see how this holds up with more play, I'm pretty confident though. I seen some small things that could maybe use a little tweaking and I see now Charles has already addressed some of these to his liking like seiges being pretty tough and cities not quite important enough to name a couple, lets see if after a few more games I also go down the tweaking road.    

Rating could go up but I dont know this is high for me, I'm certain it won't go below a 9 for sure, probably settle at 9.5 but it could just make a 10. (maybe just a small tweak here or there ....LOL) 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] - Well halfway through another game and I wonder if the cards arent going in Hannibals favour how much harder it makes it for him. I do have to say that so far I find the strategy opaque which is not at all typical for me so maybe I'm not the best judge of it right now but it seems that to give Hannibal a chance to be a pest in Italy you do need some cards.

Overall luck is definitely not LOW but I dont think its too high either, [i]maybe[/i] for the length of the game though which is maybe the bigger issue, it can go 4 hours no problem or longer in your first games. I might cut a round out or two at least until games go faster from more experience.  

I do think seiges need the tweak too, 2 instead of 3 for non-critical cities to make then a touch easier and to make them a little more tempting a small bonus for conquering like a free CU and a free flip of an adjacent PC seems fair. I also tweaked the retreat table so that you only lose the whole force if you roll an "8" (adjusted 6). Rating likely to stay where it is for good, its a fun strategic game that plays out history well

RATING: 9
Probably the high water mark for CDWGs.  Its fairly simple yet allowing for viable replay and subtle advantage for extended play.  The new Valley Games production is top-notch.  The much criticized Battle Card system works well for the period and gives a nice feel for an escalating military engagement.  The alternative would be a d6 on a CRT and would not work as well.  Basically, its a simple minigame with significant tension that rewards larger armies but provides enough information to allow strategic withdrawals.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Got at the great Underhill spring sale.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like the deck composition and the reduced variance in Ops values compared to Twilight Struggle. (TS cards range from 1-4, Hannibal cards from 1-3.) There are good hands and bad hands in Hannibal but even on a "bad" turn I never really felt screwed by my card selection.

The political control mechanic is a thematic way to simulate the effects of military conquest. It feels a little gamey in the application to the barbarian lands of France though. I suppose it's necessary for game balance but those "meaningless" PC markers are placed and retracted so quickly I have trouble making it work in my mind. This is just a quibble, as the game in general is very thematically immersive.

Nowhere is this better done than in the implementation of the generals. Hannibal is well represented as an absolute terror, capable of inflicting ruinous losses on Rome even with the odds against him.

I've decided to rate Hannibal a 9. There's so much tension in the game that I can't really see myself playing too often; usually I enjoy a mellower gaming experience. But when I'm in the mood to ratchet it up a bit, Hannibal will be on my short list.

RATING: 9
Hannibal is a wonderfully rich game of strategy, tactics, skirmishes, and diplomacy. The depth and attention to detail here are staggering. The card-driven system provides​ both players a multitude of options each turn. The historical flavor shines through brilliantly with several astute design choices (e.g., the constant shifting of Roman consuls, Carthaginian naval inferiority, etc.), allowing the players to feel immersed in the time period. Chance is a major factor, so learning to naviagte risk and mitigate the random elements in your favor is part of the challenge and beauty of the game. A monumental achievement in the political/war genre.

RATING: 8
For the most part, it's extravagantly, sinfully well-produced. I've come to dislike the map though--it's just so busy, and in places like southern Italy it's tough to distinguish province borders from the point-to-point connections. On top of that, new players are bamboozled by which provinces are politically significant and which aren't, because the map doesn't assist in any way. The rulebook unfortunately contains a host of sloppy typographical errors (par for the course from Valley Games) and is near useless for actually learning the game due to brain-dead organization. There are also some Strategy Cards with lazy typos. 

Keep a close eye on your armies as they gear up for a battle--if you don't have friendly PCs to fall back to in the event of a defeat, the effects are [b]brutal[/b]. This is a pretty tough game to teach because there are just so many little rules that have a procedure of their own: avoid battle, interception, withdrawal, elephant charges, retreat, etc.

RATING: 6.5
This game tells a great narrative about the second Punic war. It has a steep learning curve and will require a learning game. Hang on, because eventually this games rewards you with flavorful stories and after every game you ponder what you've could have done different or better. For me that's a sign of a good game.   

However I do find the game length disproportionately long given the amount of luck in drawing of strategy cards, battle cards and dice results as seen for example on the retreat table. Graphically the second edition map could've been more clearer regarding the province roads and borders in Italy. There's a hefty amount of rules that add realism but complicate the game. The rule book did a bad job at explaining. For example: converting PC markers enlisted as an 'exception' in the bits & odds in chapter 19.2, really?

RATING: 9
This is an epic game of tense, asymmetrical conflict. Hannibal acts as the MVP for Carthage, and can smash into Italy in the early game. However, Rome shines in the long-run, rallying reinforcements at a faster rate. This means that Hannibal becomes more of a roaming, opportunistic warlord as the game goes on, unable to complete the conquest but very difficult for the Romans to defeat decisively. It's a cool trade-off that presents each side with a different set of challenges. I'll have to play more to assess the subtleties of the event cards, but so far the deck impresses. Recommended for anyone who has 3-4 hours and wants to give CDGs a try. Oh, and it's gorgeous.

RATING: 9
Very impressed with this design. The battle cards actually work reasonably well - they impart a narrative to the engagements while the counterattack mechanism takes into account the relative skills of the commanders, giving the feeling that the better general gets his chance to shine.

RATING: 8.5

RATING: 10
I've played this about 6 times, but in a  every-other-month, distracted-Dads kind of way.  My opponent is a Rome freak ("SQPR" tattoo on his ankle), so I almost always play Carthage.

I love the way this game punishes sloppy play, especially for Carthage.  That feels authentic.  The risk for Carthgage of being lost at Sea is very sobering, as is the problem of supply.

I learned quickly that getting Hannibal across the Alps is only the beginning -- the real question is, can you keep him supplied with soldiers, while the Romans bring their superior regenerative ability to bear?

Unfortunately, the answer to that question for me has been "no."  Every time I invade Italy, I lose.  Every time I pick a smaller battle -- for Sardinia and Corsica, or for Sicilia -- I have much better results.

UPDATE 5/06: I finally managed a successful invasion on Italy -- what a rush!  It came down to one thrilling battle as my Hannibal attempted to cross from northern to southern Italy to receive reinforcements.  Memorable.
