=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I bought this and Washington's War at the same time and they are basically the same design.  My favorite genre of games is the CDG and this game is another example of why.  You have to make tough choices to play more powerful event cards for the more powerful op points or vice versa.  The combat is quite a unique system where you draw a certain # of battle cards based on certain strengths in battle (units, generals, controlled areas, strateg cards, etc) and then play cards in sequence with an attempt to match your opponents cards.  Initiative in battle is absolutely key as you can only win a battle if you have the initiative.  So generals with low ratings can be quite a liability.  The game is extremely tactical and units are in very short supply so decisions need to be made very carefully.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Valley Games Reprint (2007): The typos were annoying, especially the one on the map board itself. Some of the design decisions were also questionable. However, the fact that Hannibal is in my hands *right now* and not sitting in my wishlist next to other OOP titles that are out of my price range on eBay helps alleviate most of the bad feelings brought on by the former.

The game itself is wonderful, especially if you enjoy the period it covers. The system does a fine job of modeling various aspects, from Rome's superior navy, to the fickle whims of the Senate fearing the very men they charged with defending Rome and all the other juicy bits in between like elephant charges, brutal sieges, withering attrition, and opportunistic allies. The nice thing is that the chrome isn't overdone. Just the right amount to allow me to feel I'm really marching some elephants over the alps into the maw of the waiting Roman legions.

The game is a bit complex, as it shares many traits with the typical wargame. However, the investment is worth it if you're looking for a game that'll consume 2 to 4 hours of your life without you even noticing. Once you play your first game everything should click into place. If you've played wargames before, this will be a breeze. If you've played a MB GameMaster game like Axis and Allies, this will be a bigger bite than you're used to, but not much, and the flavor is totally worth it.

The luck in the game is from the luck of the draw and the luck of the die roll. While there is the chance you can get some crummy hands, no single hand should totally devastate a player unless they're already in a situation where even a good hand would still make them sweat. The decisions you can make each turn not only levels out the luck typically, but also lends to the feeling that while you cannot control history, you can at least try to direct its turbulent nature to do your bidding

Right now I'm giving this game a 10. The game length and number of players will always be a limiting factor of how often it will hit the table, but when given the chance I'll try to make sure and see that it does.

RATING: 8
Only played once so far. I was Hannibal, and I managed to haul ass through the Alps and invade Italy on the first round. After a failed campaign in Sicilia and a disasterous attrition roll on what would have been my final foray with elephants across the Alps, I managed to get Hannibal killed (er, sorry, "displaced") just before Scipio Africanus would have shown up. What concerns me most about this game is that I think it leads you by the nose a little bit. I'm not sure how much strategic flexibility there is when it the naval roll modifiers so carefully guide you to take over Sicily while mounting a campaign out of Cisalpinia. It's a lot of fun; an elegant, simple system, with a battle card mechanic that spares us the tedium of too much hand management. I'd love to give it an 8 or 9, but I'd need to see how it shakes out over multiple plays. This game should also get a shout-out for its stunning map, and simple, illustrative art. The generals have unique advantages that do a great job of giving each a distinct personality.

RATING: 5
At the beginning I have been fascinated with this game giving strong 8 and thinking about 9. It has a complex, but well designed rules. Game components quality is very high as well. 

When playing first time it gives great pleasure. You have a feeling that you are commanding great ancient army and the outcome of the war depends on you.

After next few games I have a feeling that this game is not about taking continuous advantage and winning at the final. It is only about making good use of cards you have. Your ideas have almost no impact on the game flow. Just the cards you and your opponent draw are important.

Mainly cards and dice rolls are changing the game, not player actions. You simple have a luck or not. The game is much too long for this. You could get your cards for the turn and know that that are poor, so next 30 min - 1h you have to watch what your opponent does. Even worse, after half of the game you can know that probably you lost but you have to play to the end next 3 hours...

This game excellently simulates the war - unpredictable events can change advantage to disadvantage or won battle to lost. But playing the board game is not entirely about simulation, it's also about fun and, in my opinion, reasonable awarding good decisions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is a really impressive CDG, unlike other games in its genre the rules are solid and without any pointless chrome, the cards are all reasonably balanced (as in there aren't many which you will never ever see played as an event) and the playing time is reasonable. 
The political game is what makes this brilliant. The end of year political control check maintains a high level of tension from beginning to end and the political penalty for losing battles is also excellent.

The tension in Hannibal still has nothing on that found in Paths of Glory though, which I find to be more exciting and satisfying overall. If you Like Hannibal and can find time for a 8-10 hour game, be sure to give Paths of Glory a go!

RATING: 9.5
I have long heard this game praised as the best card driven war game. As far as my limited experience goes, I can say that I agree.  It manages to provide an abundance of period flavor while still retaining an elegant system.  I personally like the battle card system very much as it seems to lend the game a little more tension compared to the typical roll your dice and check the CRT format.  Tension, agonizingly difficult decisions, historical flavor and the ability to control well-balanced asymmetrical powers, this game falls nicely into my wargaming sweet spot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Much has been said about this game and looks like it is not a hype.  I missed the game when it was available because of the unfamilarity about the Roman history at the time.  Many ancient games followed and I was exposed more to the period and my interest about this game grew.  Some key concepts are demonstrated in ancient warfare: walled cities and sieges, leadership (movement with the use of cards), tribes and revolts, naval movement and even political control (which is a deciding factor to the victory conditions).  The game possesses a tense (albeit unrealistic) combat system while both sides try drawing out opponent's hand of battle cards.  Results of the battle was scaled back into historical ends according to the number of cards played, meaning even you and your opponent play out all your cards (up to 20 rounds, one card one round) in extended number of rounds, you could still loose only 2 to 3 strength points.  Imagine how impossible an ancient army can change from frontal assault to a left or right flank in an instant.  The avoidance before combat, withdrawals during the combat, displacements of generals and combat units, interception and political control modelling makes this a fluid and interactive game. The timing to come to a battle has never been more important in this game.  You can be on the strategic offensive in one year, and on a defensive one next year.  Hannibal really tests your generalship of what and where to go in a suitable timing to the fullest extent.  The second edition has a substantial update and clarification than the first one.  It shows how many possibilities that this game could have offered which cannot be covered completely by the original rulebook.  I am a little suprised by the popular reception of this game as the game is very abstract to a high degree to most non-wargamers.  Nonetheless, the game mechanics accomodate a lot of the complex operations and concept in a very efficient way.  Every time I play, I have a good feeling about it - a sense of academic accompolishment.  The system is a little bit on the gamey side, but yet it draws you to deep thinking about what the war was about in ancient time.  It proves to be yet another success from Mark Simonitch. A very tense but fun game which, after all, reflects the historically manuveoring tactics adopted by both sides.  A highly recommended item. Valley Games edition in June 06 $65.

RATING: 4
[Played 7 times]
It's long, complex (several rules and exceptions and exceptions to the exceptions...) and involving a lot of luck (dice and cards), but also some strategy and some tactic.
The ebb-and-flow of luck grants the possibility to recover, but also reduces the rewards for skillful play.
Since it's for 2 players, it hasn't two of the worst flaws of all wargames: player elimination and coalition against someone (the leader, the weakest, the unlucky...).
In the end, it requires too much time (to learn it and then play it) for an outcome so heavily affected by luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Rating based on 2 players.

This game lies right on the edge of the amount of complexity I can handle somewhat comfortably (though still a bit incompetently at first). For my tastes, there are too many modifiers, small rules, and exceptions; however, all these rules do make sense thematically, and while there is a number of small chits to move around I would say the game doesn't feel fiddly overall. 

My favourite part of this game is definitely the battling system which uses Battle Cards, the number of which you receive is based on your army strength + modifiers, and which you must use to match your opponent's attack before you have a chance to roll for initiative and make your own attack. It's a fun way to give someone a definite advantage while still allowing for lucky escapes and mild strategy, and is more exciting than simple dice rolls. It also increases the flavour of the theme, as more powerful generals can gain the upper hand even if they have a much smaller army. I don't think this would work in a game with more players as it would result in a lot of downtime for non-battlers (like in Titan), but it works very nicely for a 2-player game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Just brilliant!!!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
What this game is good at is generating tension: the battle card system in particular can be absolutely excruciating, and general card play forces you into tough choices between competing priorities. It also enjoys the distinction of being one of the few conflict games I've come across in which you can properly attempt to bluff through your play choices and not just through banter. It does, however have a number of downsides. The rules complexity is a bit of a nightmare IMO, pushing the limit of what I'd consider realistically playable. The game also seems very capricious - a single event card or die roll can sometimes effectively decide the entire game and it generally seems subject to the whims of fate more often that the heavyweight rules and analysis can carry. In summary, when it works it's excellent and when it doesn't it can be rather dull, and it fails to work properly during a significant minority of plays. See my review for more details: http://fortressat.com/index.php?option=com_content&view=article&id=311

RATING: 8.5
After reading the reviews on this one, it became a buy-before-play game (which I usually don't do).  And I am very happy with my decision.  Hannibal plays smooth, and relatively fast.  It requires a reasonable amount of planning and thought - more on turn-by-turn basis I think, rather than a whole-game strategy (much of one's play is in reaction to your opponents play - much more so than in many wargames.  You'll see what I mean when you play).  THis is ok though.  Also, this game really fills a nitch.  It is fairly deep in analysis and planning, but is playable in ~3-5 hours.  This is rare for a wargame, expecially for one that is as entertaining as Hannibal.  The only real downside is the randomness associated with the fact that it is a CDG.  So far it really hasn't been an issue (in my 4 plays), but it certainly could be.  We made a house rule allowing for re-draws if a player gets 4 or more 1's in his hand - and this seems to work well (I know, it's not perfect, but it beats having a shit-hand, and being able to do next to nothing for a full turn). 
Any gamer who likes CDGs should give this game a try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Having taken college Latin and being a history junkie, I can say that this game perfectly captures the flavor of the times. From the consul selection mechanisms, to the slow but steady Fabius, to the tribal subjugation and sieges, the game has most everything one would want. 

Mechanics-wise, the game is very easy to learn and teach, even to people who are not wargamers. The battles are tense, since the outcomes can be very variable - just like what actually happened!

The better player, if they get 'normal' cards + rolls, can probably win this game 80+% of the time.  Of course, you can get hosed with bad consul pulls/retreat roles/events, but similar to poker the best players can control their luck enough of the time that at WBC the best players often end up with at least 3+ wins.

If there is any criticism I might have of the game, it's that depending on the situation there can be a severe slowdown of the game on turn 7+. If lots of armies are on the board and italy/spain/africa are locked down, it will be almost impossible for either side to break through, and the fighting will center on one particular province (corsica usually). This leads to a tiresome and predictable 3 turns, and often whoever gets better campaign cards can sail enough troops to take/hold the province on turn 9.

The battle cards also make the game interesting, but I often wonder if smaller battles should have a simpler resolution mechanism to make the game a bit shorter.  Something like a die chart for battles where the card ratio would be 3:1 or more, with attacker/general bonuses factored in. But honestly, this is very nitpicky. If the player whose attack it was shuffles the deck immediately afterwards, it can move along very quickly.

RATING: 9
One of the first CDG (Card Driven) wargames, quite unique back then (1996) - We the People hit the market only 2 years before.
Just read the cards (simple, to-the-point instructions), then plan your actions and try to win the 2nd punic war either with military or political pushes. 

A very unique design, combat isn't dice-based instead you have a card minigame, where players use a dedicated deck to outsmart the opponent. This is a very clever twist that impressed its mark in other non-wargames too (like Middle-earth Quest).

RATING: 2
Ugh, horrible random mess - feel guilty for selling it, should have burned it O.o
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This reminds me of another GMT production: Mark Herman's "Washington's War," in that both games are designed by talented, clever people, but published by different game companies that in both cases produced rulebooks that are so badly written and especially, organized, that they make the games significantly harder to learn than they should be. 

With "Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage," if you're going to get this game you'd better be ready to commit much energy to learning the rules (lots of information to absorb, and, in my case, rewriting and reorganizing them) and in particular, playing the game repeatedly in order to see how it's really supposed to play and to figure out strategies. 

In my opinion, that's a significant, long-term commitment. This is a perfect example of how NOT to write a rulebook and why rules should be written conversationally--albeit precisely--as though explaining the game to one person. It's just a RULEBOOK--not a sociological treatise. From what I've read, the rulebook for this former Avalon-Hill title wasn't rewritten much from the original, and it shows. The best thing about the rulebook for this game is that Valley Games used 1 1/2 spaces between lines, which gives me room to interject plain English. 

Also, the organization is abysmmal: I count 65 instances of cross-references that require jumping at least one page to find--and not once, in either direction, frontward or backward, is there a reference to which page. Plus, the rulebook was poorly proofread, with typos and mistakes throughout--more mistakes the more I look at it closely. 

This is completely aside from the game design itself, which is obviously outstanding. And the rules themselves aren't that difficult to understand--it's just that there is a lot to keep in mind, and there's no way to do that upon first plays. I see that many people rate the game as "heavy" and I don't blame them: I would have too, until after I totally rewrote and reorganized the rule book. After that undertaking, I rate the rules as 'medium-heavy', which in my book means 'a bit daunting to take on and learn but with an extra effort I can probably learn the game, assuming a significant time commitment.' In any case, it's a major undertaking to try to learn and play this game; people will have to decide whether it's worth the effort. Personally, I reckon it is, based on overall ratings and my own limited experience in playing it (as of January 2017).

In my first attempt to learn the game the rulebook stopped me as surely as Hannibal backed by elephants. I purchased this new in May of 2009, and by early March 2010 I'd completed a total rewrite and reorganization of the rule book. I then fine-tuned my 'rules in plain English' version with additional plays. 

Rulebook aside, the production by Valley Games is absolutely gorgeous, and the game looks fantastic when set up. It's also an intriguing design. The use of cards to resolve combat may not be to everyone's taste, but I don't have a problem with it. I expect I could spend years and years trying to exhaust the strategic and tactical possibilities, and given the complexity of the game, that's probably exactly how it will 'play out' for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Comment after one session:

Very tense and exciting play: The winner was decided during the last turn, which is always a good thing. Three turns before, the winner was in complete disarray and thought he couldn't win!

I particularly enjoyed the smoothness of play. The Battle system also gives a nice flavor of the period, while being more subtle and less luck dependent than a single dice roll.

An impressive GAME on the Second Punic War: Highly recommended for Ancient and CDG players!

RATING: 9
This is a greaT game! 

In Hannibal there are 2 planes of struggle: one political and one military. You influence regions and you fight your opponent's armies.  One of the clever things about this game is the way both subgames are integrated and influence each other. When you loose a battle, there are repercussions on the political plane and that's great!

I really enjoy the CDG mechanic. You get a lot of historical flavour without too much added complexity, it allows for clever tactical play and is always a source of difficult decisions.

Playtime is around 3/4 hours for us but I suspect it can decrease with experience. That's not too much, for a game like this.

Good game. Might not need. Consider trading.

Good concept with some interesting innovations - determining the outcome of battles by playing manoeuvre/tactics cards is a lovely idea, but at the same time introduces quite an element of luck. Having played it a couple of more times now, I'm still of the opinion that the battle card mechanic is the shortcoming of the game.  I like all the other elements of it except that.

RATING: 5
Despite only 2 partial plays (4 hours to play 4 game years in one game), I feel I can make some valid comments: firstly, this is one game that you need committed players to spend the time and effort. Sadly, I'm time poor, and the rules and mechanics are unweildy mentally and physically with only time giving you some mastery. Still I find this design provides a stronger political and dramatic narrative than other similar designs: 1960 the Making of the President's political system is incremental via cubes, whereas in Hannibal political control is done by tile flipping. Also the card effects translate better in Hannibal, than either 1960 and Twilight Struggle (there was no real feel of espionage in TS, whereas this conveyed far better in Cold War: KGB vs CIA). Also because of the small card manifest which is listed means there is more chance of anticipating events, which is difficult in 1960 and TS. The other plus in Hannibal is the interesting spatial considerations and exciting interaction of the battles, but again it is still a fiddly process. I really enjoy this series of games using the historical "what if" event deck as the central mechanic as I feel I am learning, and look forward to its huge potential being mined for more streamlined designs playable in an evening.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Your first outing here is probably going to be a slow affair as you reference rules and deal with each situation for the first time. All following games are a blast! More so if you read up on and can appreciate the history behind the game. The designer has done a fantastic job of bringing the history of the Second Punic war to a board game and yet creating a balanced and fun experience. 

While I can't help feeling that everything about the game is trying hard to push me down a set path, I've learned to appreciate that. After all, I'm playing the Second Punic War and so there's reasons behind why each side did what they did. Those reasons are cleverly woven into the game through modifiers, abilities of different generals, attrition and sea movement, etc.  I've come to appreciate a steered path over pure randomness.

I've also learned to really appreciate the cleverness of the battle cards. Initially I thought they were a pain - like they are in We the People - but they've been refined here into an often tense battle simulation. Generals can play the odds, give up initiative for the extra glory of the Double Envelopment, bluff with a reserve card, try and drag out the battle for extra battle losses, etc. There's some nail biting moments for sure.

This is a game I'm looking forward to playing time and time again. The theme is one I'm interested in and one the game has made me more interested in. Rules are a bit fiddly if you haven't played in a while, so I'll be sure of getting this to the table at least every six months - it deserves it.

The Valley Games board is very hard to read in Italy - brown borders on brown background. Otherwise (despite the -1 minor Alps typo), it's nice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Uses the [b]We the People system[/b], including battle cards. The Carthaginians operate on a knife-edge and can't afford to lose a single major battle or too many units, since most of their reinforcements begin in Hispania or Africa, far away from Italy. However, the Carthaginians do get the most powerful general in the game, Hannibal, with a large army at the very start. Because of the inability to easily replace losses, they tend to avoid battle and spend most of their time pillaging Italy.

The Romans have weak leaders at first, but can raise endless legions of troops. In late game, the general Scipio Africanus arrives with a large army. He is the only Roman general to be the equal of Hannibal. The strategy card "Spy in Enemy Camp", allows you to examine half of your opponent's battle cards, which is ridiculously overpowered.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
Simonitch takes the superb and innovative We the People CDG system and cranks it up one level. With the setting as the second punic war, it's already half the battle won. The cards are now utterly more useful and can be used as an event or ops card (activate generals, place PC markers, convert PC markers).The gameplay is very tense and there are so many agonising decisions to be made. Each side has a different set of strategies and with the cards, each game can be vastly different. This system is elegant and I believe will stand up to repeated playings.Own unpunched AH version from the last days (sticky taped shut instead of shrinkwrapped). Still has Hobbytown USA sticker on it for $40.

Update: Sold original copy. Preordered VG version.

RATING: 9.5
Almost bloody perfect.

RATING: 8
Hannibal is an awesome system. It has aged surprisingly well. Learning the rules took a few games, but Hannibal just seems the capture the right balance of game and simulation. Abstracting history is a tremendous challenge, and I marvel at how well this game has succeeded. There is just enough asymmetry to make playing both sides exciting and different, and not so much that the conflict is unbalanced. Each game is filled with tension...can you tell I haven't played this for awhile? I enjoy the game, but it does require a good 3 hours to play, and it's often a challenge to find that much time for a 2 player game. Also, Hannibal's rules are *just* complex enough that I know I will have to relearn 50% of the rules next time I bust this out. So, those are my two gripes about the game. Even so, this one is a keeper for me, and I look forward to future plays. Valley Games also did a very fun publishing job, insofar as the materials are concerned. The board comes together like a giant jigsaw, the cards and counters are all high quality, and eventually, even my miniature generals arrived!

Great CDG about the II Punic War. Beautiful map of point to point system. Components of good quality and great esthetics.
All cards events really happened, giving a historical background. Medium difficulty to be a wargueim, 9 turns which require a half hour to complete.
The combat system is original: Resolved by Battle Cards, approx. 25 -75% & Strategy/Luck respectively. Is one of the strengths of the game, but prolongs the runtime.

Régóta rajta volt a kipróbálási listámon, nagy reményekkel vágtam neki a TS elődnek. Abszolút nem csalódtam! Az egyik játékos Római Birodalmat, a másik a karthágóiakat irányítja. A cél Róma, illetve Karthágó elfoglalása, illetve ha ez nem sikerült 9 forduló alatt, akkor a játék végén, aki több tartományt birtokol azé a győzelem, a döntetlen Karthágónak kedvez, ami jelen esetben nagyon sokat számít, ugyanis a játék jó játék esetén végig kiegyenlített marad. Első játékunkban egyszer sem vezetett egyik fél sem 3-al. A két félnek teljesen más a stratégiája. Lehet a történelmet követni és Hannibállal átkelni az Alpokon, de teljesen felül is lehet írni a múltat. A karthágóiak főleg hadvezérekben erősek, a rómaiak utánpótlásban és lapok terén. Nagyon jól megoldott a csata, talán az egyik legjobb, amivel találkoztam. 6 különböző lap, ebből egy dzsóker, amit csak a harcra használunk. Annyi az egész, hogy amíg a védekező fél tud ugyanolyan kártyát kirakni, mint a másik, addig tartja magát, aki először nem tud rakni vagy nem ugyanolyat az veszít. Természetesen a kezdő lapszám függ a találkozó seregek és hadvezérek mennyiségétől és szintjeitől. Egyszerű, mégis tökéletesen működik. Szép és jó a térkép felosztása, elég jó kiegyenlített a két fél, bár Rómát kicsit erősebbnek érzem. Egy dologba tudok csak belekötni, de ezt is lehet több játék után másképp látnám, hogy az egyes eseménylapok közt vannak nagyon erősek, ami egy teljes provinciát tudnak elvenni az egyik féltől, ami rengeteget számíthat. Nagyon egyben van ez a játék, abszolút megérdemli a top100-ban a helyét. Persze hosszú és a szabálya sem annyira letisztult, mint a TS-é, de nagyon jó játék.

RATING: 8.5

Enterainment Value: Good 2.5 out of 4

Bad: The combat system is novel and quick and strategic, but perhaps more strategy aimed at counting cards and poker than any war tactics.

RATING: 8
Great theme, nice movement system, great use of contol markers (for losing battles , weathering and popular opinion). Superior to We the People in that cards are much more balanced and you aren't stuck with a hand you can't use (although I kind of miss the othello like quality that the control markers had in We the People).  While I do think this is an excellent game I don't think that this is worth the price that it is going for on ebay (and here on the geek), this is at most a $70 game (and that is stretching it.  Still, it evokes the theme and mood quite well while remaining abstract enough not to get bogged down in the Berg-like details.  My only complaint that perhaps it is a bit too long for what it is (but, I'm not a huge fan of two player war games). Deserves a reprint.

RATING: 6
What sets this apart from regular wargames is the political control system; the cleverest maneuvering of armies accomplishes little and can be downright dangerous if you don't pay equal attention to winning over the locals. There's a lot else to like about this game, but I find it hard work to play, requiring frequent searches through the rule book for the first couple of games at least, and always a lot of die rolls and table-consulting. It doesn't help that sometimes negative factors are bad, and sometimes they're good, so that you forget whether you're trying to roll high or low. Call me crazy, but I sold my copy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
As far as I'm concerned, this is the gold standard of an elegant CDG.  I was a little unsure of the battle cards concept at first, but now whole-heartedly support them for this game.  

Anyone with a nodding understanding of the second punic war would realize that it pitted Hannibal's tactical genius against the overwhelming strategic advantages of the Romans.

By necessity, tactical games on the war would have to tilt "pro-Carthage", while strategic level games would inevitably tilt "pro-Roman".  Trying to do both leads to a freakin' monster - probably unplayable.

The battle cards do a very nice job of abstracting the tactical combat while allowing the tactical genii - Hannibal and Scipio - to overcome long odds against lesser lights.

RATING: 9.5
A great two-player wargame. I think it's got all the elements that you want in one. The use of the political control markers shift back and forth much like Twilight Struggle (yet the wargame aspect of it makes this game much more interesting). It encourages spreading out of troops in order to gain that control. Combat is awesome - the longer it goes, the more both sides lose, but the loser ends up always taking some significant losses. I love how there are tactics cards in battles, and a lucky draw can get you a win there, but often probabilities hold the day. It just might be the best two-player wargame out there.

RATING: 7.5
Only one play, and a few read throughs of the rules.  I feel I can pretty adequately assess this game as it compares to Twilight Struggle, Successors, and 1960...  it's very good, between TS and 1960 in terms of my enjoyment (TS being one of my very favourite games, period, 1960 being one I just like - -RvC is closer to TS than 1960).  It's probably closest to Successors, which I also quite like -- it's very close to being the 2-player game on which Successors is built, I guess.

It is not as tense as TS, and the card decisions are rarely as immediately agonizing, two factors that push TS over the top into "legendary" status for me.  Still, the battle resolution mechanic of RvC is sweet, and the game itself is beautiful.  My only quibble is that sea-movement seems soooooo integral to me, yet you can be hosed in a huge way by a single roll -- that's very swingy, and while historically accurate might not make for the best games in some situations.  I think those would be rare, however.

RATING: 7
Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage, after 1 play, rating: 7
Well, I have seen the mechanics before (Paths of Glory, Twilight Struggle, 1960: The Making of the President and so on) and I like them, especially the battlecard part is really good (if the battles are large). I am also a big fan of Rome and that part of the history. I have even read a large Hannibal book a year ago. AND (not but) I like this game. It was a tense experience, and I won so what could I complain about? 
I do not like the victory condition. Why not? Because you are in a disadvantage if you have to attack, but you win the game if you control more then half of the map. So you take a little more then half the map and then let your opponent attack your stronger position. I would like a victory condition that forced you to be more offensive, both as Romans and as Hannibal.
Oh I don’t really know... Might have to play the game some more times.

RATING: 9
Solid game.  I have not played a lot of CDG but I can tell this one is special.  Seems fairly balanced, although I feel the cards favor Carthage.  But that may just have been my one play and looking at it through (what is the opposite of rose) colored glasses. 

The game is tight; but there can be wild swings in fate.  Many of the events are extremely situational, but if the situation exists, they can be hugely powerful.  So these events are really hit or miss.  Then there is the fate of the dice, where there are situations where you roll for losses (attrition, retreat, crossing the alps) and the one die roll will result in you losing from 0%-50% of your army.  A bad roll here can cripple your ability to wage war.

RATING: 6.75
Very tedius. The game is very beautiful (to look at), but it is not very practical in its component design which isn't optimized for actual gameplay. I like that things are good-looking, but I also like that it is easy to decipher. For instance it should be possible to obtain relevant information by just LOOKING at the gameboard (like an armys size). This game has some problems in this regard. The rulebook could also be better, and the gameboard actually has an embarrasingly printing error on it. Be sure too check a FAQ or errata before you play.

The game in itself is a quite good 2-player, and recommendable. There is definitely a big luck-factor, and sometimes single-standing disasters can ruin the game for either site (For example Hannibal losing his first battle with double envelope and being wiped of the board). Despite this it actually remains a quite good game. War is chancy business! 

The game seems well-balanced between Rome and Carthage.

RATING: 8.3
A major step forward in the design of Card-Driven-Games, because it introduced the concept of Operation Points which made every card playable (even if it had your opponent's event on it, which was not yet the case in We the People).
Very balanced with lots of games decided on the very last turns. I really liked the battle cards concept, although it added to the game length.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Well, it IS a great game.  I had fond memories of it from the '90s, and purchased the new VG edition.  Lovely work!  And a really good game.  I can see what people mean when they say you can "formulate a plan for the turn" when looking over your hand.  It's far more true in this game than in a lot of CDGs, where things like supply issues can really force your hand (and force you to change your plans for your hand).  Partly it's because your replacements are preprogrammed, whereas in games like PoG you're always trying to create a window for yourself to take replacements.

Worries?  Well, it's a pretty straightforward situation--I don't know how much strategic flexibility either side has.  That means that the game may have a certain sameness over time.  Worse than, say, War of the Ring?  I don't know.  Hopefully time will tell!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Card-driven wargame in the mould of 'We the People'. 'Hannibal' takes the main features of the aforementioned game and develops upon them thereby becoming a truly great game in its own right. Cards can now be played for their operations points or to trigger certain events, and the placing of political control markers has become increasingly strategic and less of a tit for tat affair. The introduction of combat cards, where opponents receive a number of cards dependent on their combat strength and these are played until one side is unable to match the cards played by the other, makes battles far more tense occurrences. Overall, this is an extremely deep and strategically rewarding game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Arnaud, Mike, Vincent
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There really isn't much to say about this game that hasn't already been said by the others who have also assigned this game a high score. This is the card-driven gaming system at its peak; a tense game with just the right blend of chance and planning, and constantly difficult decisions. The battle card system may slow down the game a bit, but provides the game with a capacity for upsets that one could not get using a standard CRT - and is therefore well worth it. The best 2-player game in my collection; if not the best 2-player wargame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Good card driven wargame that is as much tactical as strategic. Overall game strategy is clear for both sides (carthage: rush to North Italy, secure and split off your seafaring general to keep forces occupied and free him for your carthage stack after the reshuffle, use your superior mobility to put hannibal in south eastern Italy taking multiple provinces when the time (i.e. your hand) is right. Rome: keep hannibal and aupport in liguria and prepare for the push towards spain), but it is the tactics that both drive your hand as well as the timing aspects of the overall strategy. Is travel out of Africa still permitted? Does my opponent have a storm when I need to make a crossover? Is hannibal still well enough supplied or should I take my foot off the pedal? Can I already start putting pressure to Spain? Is sicily about to be invaded?

You'll find the same set of moves being performed across multiple games, though not all and at different timing. And it is these last elements, coupled with a pretty cool battle mechanism which even adds in a bluff element, that keeps the game involved at every turn.

Add good theme and you have a great game. Why only 7.5 then? After some games I do start to wonder about the replay value. Certainly not something I expected from a game that is touted as the "best strategy game". More experience will tell.

Great game? Yes. Worth owning? Yes. Go out on a limb to on it? Not necessary.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Mark Simonitch had hit the bulleyes!  The political game and battle resolution using cards are inherited from "We the People", but when Simonitch tied the Operation and Event elements into one Strategy card, than the card-driven mechanics is finally mature.
Now each card represent some resource from your empire, you can activate your general (supply troop), hire mercenaries, do some intrigues (spying, bribing enemy allies), diplomacies (naval reinforcement from other country), or even pray for gods (weather, luck, etc).  Your hand management now represents resource management, and playing which card in which order may affect your initiative.  As a result, every card played is a hard decision that require planning, bluff and of course some luck. 
This game also excel that it has only 3 to 4 stacks of armies per side and can be completed under 4 hours, how many wargame can claim to have that many strong points?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After a couple of plays, the only thing I can compare it to is Twilight Struggle and its card driven tense-ness. Combat is random, but weighted and usually won by the better prepared army. It feels fun, but the event cards can really swing the game and in a long tight game, a player can lose in the last turn through no fault of their own if the other guy gets really good cards. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I've now received my copy from Valley Games, and this is a provisional rating after a couple of plays (on wargameroom.com), both of which I greatly enjoyed.  Hannibal is a wonderful mix of maneuver and combat, with politics and area control: concentrating only on winning every battle, or neglecting clever positioning of your forces, will clearly lose you the game.  While the rules are not as clear and concise as they might be, Hannibal is a lighter wargame that plays relatively quickly (1.5-2 hours for my first plays online, so I could easily see games between experienced players taking two hours 'face to face', and 60-75 minutes online).  Like many card-driven wargames, there seems to be quite a bit of luck involved.  Battles especially - in my opinion, this is particularly appropriate in a game of ancient warfare - are not known quantities, and while it pays to play the odds, there's plenty of room for inferior forces, played smartly with fortunate draws, to win the day.

The Valley Games reprint of this game is without a doubt the most visually attractive, and highest production value, wargame, and for that matter, perhaps even boardgame, that I own.  (My only minor complaint with the map is that the lines of the two straits can be difficult to see in certain qualities of light.)

RATING: 8
I prefer [i]War of the Ring[/i] and [i]Twilight Struggle[/i] as far as 2-player CDGs go, but this one also has a permanent place on my shelf due to the fun, unique battle system (that more games should borrow from) and a high incentive for conflict.

As far as the Valley Games edition goes, I think the rules are a hot mess -- definitely more convoluted than they need to be -- though the cheat sheet is pretty good.  The rules are also full of careless typos.  The quality of the components are extremely good, but I'm not a fan of the early-to-mid-2000s CGI which already looked extremely dated the day the game went to print.  And the map is overly busy with indistinct borders.  Overall, a disappointing production even though you tell the publisher tried really, really hard to make it look amazing.

Fun game that I'd like to become more familiar with.  There's definitely a learning curve, though that's true of pretty much any game of this complexity.

RATING: 9
Great gameplay full of tension and suspence.

Historically good but some critics :
- the sieges are not interesting enough and are not pursued in the game (in the Second Punic War, 7 of the 12 cities have been stormed)
- The Double Envelopment is used a lot in the real war (Trebia, Trasimene, Cannae, but also Ilipae by Scipio and other battles less known) but in the game, the automatic loss of initiative makes it too dangerous to be really interesting, even for Hannibal.
- Overseas ventures should be more tempting for the Romans as in the real war

It can probably be improved by the variants of Charles Féaux de la Croix (Variant 1 : alternate siege rules, alternate setup, but New Carthago and Gades would not enjoy special status as they fell rather easily in the actual war). I'd also like to give to the best generals an advantage to play Double Envelopment which I think is too dangerous to be worth the risk actually and has the same effect as Hannibal faces Varro or Scipio Africanus. An example could be that the initiative is to be rolled but the defender can double his tactical rating in case of a Double Envelopment played, which makes its use easier when faced with a bad general like Varro and seems historical : every Double envelopment who succeeded in the war was against a lousy general (variant 2)
Addendum 15/05/10 : la variant 2 est trop avantageuse pour Carthage. Variante 3 : idem variante 1 mais les engins de siège carthaginois commencent à Carthago Nova (si contrôlé et non assiégé) plutôt qu'avec un général carthaginois. Ils peuvent passer la mer mais pas les cols. Compte comme 1 unité pour le mouvement naval.

Variante 1 : favorise un peu Carthage (à privilégier)
Variante 2 : favorise beaucoup Carthage
Variante 3 : équilibrée

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game lives up to its reputation as a wargaming classic. There's much talk about how Hannibal is a shining star of the Card-Driven genre, a genre that I generally don't care for. But what makes this game great isn't the cards - it's the balance and decision-making of the gameplay. You don't come away from the game thinking in terms of cardplay like you do in Twilight Struggle (which is still a fine game). The cards keep the game moving forward by reducing Analysis Paralysis and adding historical flavor through events, but what you remember about a session are your strategic choices. The ability of this game to pack so much strategy into a 2-3 hour package is what's most impressive, and what will keep this game coming back to the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a fun war game for 2 players.  One person takes Rome with Italy and the islands in their control and the other takes Carthage with Africa and Spain in it's control.  The goal is to have more political influence than your opponent.  In addition there are sudden death scenarios.  If Hanibal dies or the Carthage capital is lost then Carthage loses.  If Rome looses it's city then Rome loses.  Play is controlled by situation cards which can either be used to move generals, place influence markers or events.  Battles are fought using battle cards with a simple matching mechanism.  Armies are lost during retreat and attrition after battle or attrition while crossing the mountains.  It's heavy enough to offer lots of choices and light enough that it can be taught quickly.  Overall a great game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good.  Deserving of its stature as a classic, even if I miss some of the bells and whistles that have come to the card-driven wargame genre since.  Fast-paced, but still engrossing, which is a hard combination to achieve.  The battles are faster than I was worried about, and the continuously-eroding political control system is really fantastic.  I love the asymmetric but balanced sides.  The biggest drawback to this game is that the rules take a long time to explain, much more so than the game itself warrants.  It's important to tell a new player almost all of the rules, because they almost all have a significant impact on strategy - the rules don't leave you much ability to say "There's also some more specifics about X, but we'll cover that when we get to it."  New players need to know the whole from the get-go, or else the game won't make sense.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
complicated rules, but very interactive gameplay, like historical games. Da best design among my collection

RATING: 5
I can see why this was such a grail when it was OOP- it really is the perfect card-driven war game for Euro gamers, with its relative simplicity and fluid board play. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is one of those games where I want to rate it a 7 right off the bat, because what's so good about the game works very well, but I still have some slight issues with the game, and besides, we didn't even play a complete game in our first, 4-hour game.  The two sides seem to be balanced well, and the cards seem to allow for an element of strategy, though you're still going to come across a lot of "What the heck am I going to do with THESE cards?" moments.  I'm not familiar enough with the game to know whether the game is initially in Carthage's favor, but the Carthage player certainly has more control over the generals he uses, and can maintain more certainty between rounds, which to me seems to be a slight advantage.

I'm not sure how I feel about the battle deck.  It's a clever enough way to develop a fog-of-war effect, but it can be frustrating to draw 18 cards to your opponent's 7, and then not have one of the two cards that you need to respond to the attack.  Careful planning can account for a lot of it, but no amount of planning will help against a bad draw.  Again, it's the necessary fog-of-war effect, but I'm still getting used to the frustration that comes with it.

I'd play this again.  It's just a shame that it takes so long.

1 play: 2 players

Сэр Вершитель
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent 2-player game. Historical flair is great. 

What I love about it is how you plan your objectives for the season depending on the cards you're dealt. There's a very nice balance between "this opportunity is too good to pass up" and "this is a little too risky at this time". While political control is what matters in the long run, you can turn it around with a hearty battle. Send your forces on a campaign and strike a blow, or gather resources and allies. Or do both - but which first? Sometimes the playing order of two cards make a difference. You have to guess your opponent's moves. Brilliant.

The battle mechanics are also wonderful in how close battles can be, and how you can take your chance and win even as the underdog - good risk/reward ratio, and nicely simple at the same time.

Replay value is decent. Depending on which cards you get you can pull off different strategies. Not that there are terribly many different strategies, but it's the execution that counts, and little things can matter.

Once both players are familiar with the game, it actually plays relatively fast and can end quickly if a big battle happens to be decisive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Perfect game at all. Balanced, historically faithful. The only thing I don't like the battles with cards.

With the explosion of card driven games hitting the market, I would say, "Don't buy this game" as you are able to get games that play just as well that are still in print.

RATING: 9
The father of other card-driven wargames has a character and a pace of its own. I was pleasantly surprised with the quality of the design despite (comparatively) old age. The system has its quirks but works very well as a whole. There are plenty of tough decisions to make. There's a constantly high tension level until things start to go Bad for one side. There is a fascinating landslide effect that makes recovery very difficult after some critical but not so obvious loss of balance takes place. Plenty of depth and many things to learn here.
